tertained their friends Wednesday 
evening at the A. UO. U. W. hall with a| 
Leap Year dancing party. The hall 
was charmingly decorated with pen- 
nantsfof the club colors of blue and | 
silver gray, and two cozyfcorners made | 
a delightful addition to the scheme of 
decorations. Punch was served by the 
young ladies incharge. The following 
young ladies were hostesses: Dorothy 
Montgomery, Dorothy Dunbar, Dorothy

Georgiana Garner, Jessie Garner, Mig- 
non Allen, Kmma Wocten, Constance 
Fulton. The invited guests were the

kins, Elva Staples, and Violet Fasta- 
bend, and the Mesers Alfred Gerding,

1, where 








he is engaged in war against Octavius; reasoning deductively, Cico- 
patra concludes that after all Antony is faithful to her or he would 
not call on her to aid him, and that his marriage to Octavia was 
merely a political move; she leaves at once with ber fleet of war for 
Actium. 

PART V—At Actium, while the combat rages, she perceives that 
Antony is winning; fearful that if he wins, his love for her wili 
be lost, she turns away her ships, and Antony suspecting her tieacher) 
follows after her. Away, his forces yield. His cause is lost. Arriviug 
at her palace, Cleopatra realizes her grave mistake and sends an at 
tendant to bring Antony to her so that she can explain; but Antony 
disgraced and full of rage, drives the maid away, and acting upou 
advice of several Roman generals who are still faithfu: to Kim, he 
decides to take the queen off the throne by force and inflict severe 
punishment upon her; however, Octavius has arrived al the capital with 
his forces, and the city is quickly in his hands. When Antony lea 
this, he undergoes a complete change o! heart—he is tearful that ieal 
harm will befall the queen, and, unable to combat bis infatuation. he 
goes at break of day to the palace, and meets her while she is at 
prayers and charges her with treachery She denies ali and satisfies 
him that it was her love for him that drove her off the tield at 
Actium. 

PART Vi—When Anton)’s friends, according to the arrangement 
he had made with them, appear to make Cleopatra their prisoner, be 
interferes, and in the end persuades them to join with bim io her 
defense, Reluctantly they yield and leave to gather their forces 
against the young Octavius; their task is a hopeless one, Octavius nas 
already captured the city. Hearing a false report that the queen tas 
stabbed herself to death, Antony begs Ventidius to despatch his life 
instead, the faithful old soldier, seeing that all is lost in disgrace, kiils 
himself; Antony inflicts a mortal wound upon himself just before 
Kepbren arrives from the queen with the word that he shal] escape 
to the palace; Kephren, assisted by Pharon, bears the dying Autony to 
the tomb of the queen to which she has escaped. Here, finding that 
it is impossible to win Octavius to her side, she attempts to assassinate 


him, but the plan mise by the impetuous act of Antony, who 








suddenly recovers consciousness; Antony dies, and Octavius tells the 


queen that she will go with him to Rome a prisoner; Cleopatra places 
an asp, provided by Pharon, in her bosom, and its sting causes ber 
death 

FINI?

Phil. Sheridan and Miss Rebecca Wright meet end become 
steady friends for life. George Briarson, an orphan and nephew 
of Miss Wright, is brought up by her. He is in love witha 
Southern beauty, Caroline Ashburn. 

The old house is gaily decorated to celebrate the return of 
Geor-ie, much to the disgust of Clem Shelby, a rival suitor 
George is given a warm-hearted reception. The first gun in 
the Civil War is fired, and the news spreads like wild fire. 
of the town hall, 


Clem is an ardent Southerner, but in front 
Blows are 


George publicly announces his Union proclivities. 
struck, and a duel is arranged between Clem and George 
George steals out in the morning and is seen by c 
who sees the rapier and divines its import. She follows, and 
is a witness to the duel, in which the hotheaded attack of 
Clem is met by the cooler George, who wounds his adversary 


oline, 


and erstwhile friend. 

George receives @ dispatch from his old friend, Phil Sheri- 
dan, telling him to “come and get that promised commission 4 
He goes, leavinga proud aunt anda sorrowing girl behind him. 

Incidents in the war follow hot and fast, and one engage- 
ment succeeds another. One day, old darkey Ephriam is ad 
mitted through the lines. He is selling vegetables. Sheridan 
sends the old man with a secret message to Rebecca Wright. 
The note is written on tissue, and covered with tinoil, which 
Uncle Eph puts in his mouth. In it Sheridan asks for the 
disposition of the Rebel troops. Miss Wright gives him the 
information required. 

Sheridan sends out troops to check the enemy’s advance. 
He is aroused from a hurried sleep by bad news. He hurries 
out and finds his troopers retreating in disorder, despite the 

frantic efforts of George and other officers. 

Sheridan’s bravery and personality turn the adverse tide. 
He rallies the men, inflames them, rides back, recaptures the 
guns, nnd puts the enemy to rout. During the battle the old 
enemies, George and Clem, meet in a sabre duel, George is 
wounded, and Clem is killed by the bursting of a shell. 

George is nursed to health by Caroline, and happiness 
comes with the return of peace. George receives a letter 
from Sheridan, announcing that his commission of Captain 
awaits him.

The debate between the assecdian 


and Forum societies of the Aovoria | 
high school Friday night at the First 
Presbyterian church was thoroly 
enjoyed by an audience which filled 
the large auditorium to its utmost | 
capacity. The question debated was 
“Resolved, That the Electoral Col- | 
lege should be Abolished and the! 
President of the United States elect- | 
ed by the People by Popular Vote.” | 
The affirmative of the question was 
taken by the Alfredian team, which 
consisted of Esther Carlson, Nellie 
Larsen and Margaret Kelly, while 
the Forum team, consisting of Law- 
rence Logan, Theron Skyles and 
Jessie Garner took the negative 
side of the argument . 

The judges selected were J. O. 
Erickson, C. .W Mullins and L. L. 
Tracey, who were seated in different 
parts of the house. City Superin- 
tendent Imel and Pro one 
principal of the high school, oc- 
cupied the rostrum with the de- | 
bators. 


a 


The arguments on both sides 
were well presented, and when the 
debate was ended it seemed inevi- 
table that the affirmative should 
be given the decision, as it was con- 
ceded by all that they had the best 
of the argument, but when the bal- 
lots of the judges were opened and 
read, it was found that two of them 
were in favor of the negative and 
one for the affirmative. 

Superintendent Imel announced 
that the presentation of the trophy 
would be made at another time, } 
when Mr. Wise could be present.

THE CHRISTMAS. CANDLE. 


A Rollicking Game For Yuletide Fos- 


Christmas games for Christmas day 
should be Just the Jolliest and most rol- 
licking sort one can possibly find, and 
one that will make everybody laugh 
in spite of himself is the game of the 
Christmas candle. This should be 
Played under the supervision of an 
older person. To play it after the 
fashion of the little English children 


of the time of Queen Blizabeth quite | 


a long, fat wax candle should be pro- 
vided, with one child chosen to hold 
the Ughted candle and another to try to 
blow it out. 
a few feet away from the candle, | 
which is held at the height of his 
head, and facing it. He is then blind- 
folded, turned around three times, and 
told to take as many steps as he was 
required to before, and in the direction } 
he thinks the candle to be. Then he 
blows, trying to put out the candle’s | 
flame. Perhaps the child walked} 
straight away from the candle. Any- 
way his attempts will be very funny 
indeed as he puffs out his cheeks and 
blows—probably in the wrong direction 
—and they will cause much merriment 
among the other children, Another 
way of playing this candle game is to 
use one of the tiny unburnable Christ- 
mas trees which are to be found in the 
stores now. Very small colored can- 
dies are fastened to the tree and 
lighted, and one child holds it while a 
Second blindfolded child tries to see 
how many candle flames he can blow | 
out at one attempt. A row of the om 
dinary Christmas tree candles may be! 
fastened with pins to a holly wreath | 
so that they stand upright as the 
wreath lies upon a table. As in the 
case of the tiny Christmas tree the 
blindfolded child tries to blow out the 
flames of as many candles as possible, 
A prize for the most successful blower 
will add to the fun of these candle 
games.

THE CHRISTMAS SHIP. i 


92880 O O68 9-0-8 8O-8-8- 


The Christmas ship is one of the 
funniest games of all, The children 
sit in a circle to play it One child 
who starts the game says to his neigh- 
bor: 

“The Christmas ship has come in.” 

“What did it bring you?” asks the 
next child. 

“A Jumpingjack,” says the first child 
perhaps, beginning at once to imitate | 
the hopping motions of a toy jumping- 
jack. 

The child to whom he 5s 
also play at being a jumpingj ay- | 
ing at the same time to his neighbo: 

“My Christmas ship has come in,” 

“What did it bring you?” repeats his | 
Beighbor. 

“A lion,” says the jumpingjack, tr: 
ing to continue his hopping and to roar 
at the same time. 

The third child must roar, too, as he | 
gives the child next him the ship in- | 
formation 4nd shows him whieh toy | 
he must Imitate. | 

the time a few Human jumpi 
jacks are in motion, some of the lions 
roaring, two or three trains steaming 
around the room and some little girl 
dolls trying to say mamma and papa 
the game will end in just one grand, 
hilarious frolic.

An old fashioned game known as 
“Quaker eting’ is a great favorize 
at the Yuletide ps Those who join 
take seats around the room, and one or 
more whisper in the ear of h one 
some witty or absurd thing for him to 
de All must be quiet who are not 
pla When all are ready one per 
son claps his hands, and the first one 
must proceed to do what be was told: 
the others must not speak or laugh on 
penalty of a forfeit. Each one in turn 
must act his or her part. [t must all

tenuated verbal sym- 
induce 
complexity 
quently ex: 
gerate the obfuscation of 
the mentality of the per- 
. Conversely, exp 
sions which are reduce 
to the furthermost mint- 
mum of simplification 
and compactness, besides 
contributing realistic 
verisimilitude, constitute 
a much less onerou 
handicap to the reader 


perspleacity. Observe, 
for instance, the un- 
mistakable and in- 

capable expressiveness 
of onomatopoetic, inter- 
jectional, monosyllabic 
utterances, especially 
when motivated under 
strenuous emotional cir- 
cumstances. How much 
more appealing {s their 
euphonious pulehritude 
than the preposterous 
and pretentious pompos- 
ity of elongated verblage.

Order of Exercises 


2—QUARTE a: “Still, Still With Thee” 


Still, still with Thee, when purple morning breaketh 
When the bird waketh and the shadows flee 

Fa r than morning. Lovlier than the daylight 
Dawns the sweet consciousness, I am with Thee 
\ione with Thee, amid the mystic shadows 

The solemn hush of nature newly born 

Alone ith Thee in breathless adoration 

In the calm dew and freshness of the morn 

\s in the dawning o’er the waveless ocean 

The image of the morning star doth rest 

So in this stillness Thou beholdest only 

Thine image in the waters of my breast 

Still, still with Thee as to each new born morning 
A fresh and solemn splendor still is given 

So doth this blessed consiousness awaking 
Breathe each day nearness unto Thee and Heaven 
When L soul subdued by toil to slumber 
Its closing eye looks up to thee in prayer 

s the repose be th Thy wings o’er shading, 
But sweeter still to wake and find Thee there 

So shall it be at last in that bright morning 
When the soul waketh and life's shadows flee; 
Oh. in that hour fairer than daylight dawning 
Shall rise the glorious thought, 1 am with Thee.

SOPRANO SOLO:..“Hear Us, O Father” 
(Cello Obligato,) 


Mascherino 


Hear us, O Father, now we implore Thee, 
Grant to Thy children peace from on high 
We bow before Thee, help us to love Thee, 
Grant to us ever Thy tender blessing 
O Heavenly Father, Father almighty 
Hear now Thy children, be with us always 
Hear our supplication 
We bow in adoration, 
Trusting in Thy mercy

“My Father as Thou Wilt,” 
My Father, as Thou wilt, Oh may Thy will be 
Into Thy hand of love, I would my all resign 
Thro’ sorrow or thro’ joy conduct me as Thine own 
And help me still to say ‘My Lord Thy will be done.” 
My Father as Thou wilt tho’ seen thro’ many a t

12—CONTRALTO SOLO: “Abide With Me” 
Abide with me, fast falls the even tide; 


The darkness deepens; Lord with me abide 
When other helpers fail, and comforts flee, 


Help of the helpless, O abide with me

COMMENCEMENT WEEK. 
Social interest this week 


“\she carried white roses. D 


| orchids. 


thruout the city. On Tuesday after- 


Jnoon the Class Day program of the 


High School was 
This Class Day is an innovation 
which will from its suecess this 
year become one of the commence- 
ment customs to be celebrated by 
future senior classes. The gradua- 
tion exercises proper were given on 
Friday and were a credit to the 
class of 1915. The eighth grade 
graduates from the different schools 
gave their programs together on 
Thursday afternoon at the High 
School auditorium. About forty 
pupils were graduated at this time. 
The seniors who received diplomas 
from, the high school this . year 
were: William Sigurdson, Dale How- 
ard, Martha Jackson, Ersie Math- 
ews, Myrtle Linville, 
larkey, Helen Dahlgren,

ae eannmot be valued with the gold of Ophir, With the 
precious onyx or sapphire, Whence then cometh wisdom? 
And where is the place of understanding? 
Doth not wisdom cry, And understanding put forth her 
voice? 
In the top of high places by the way, Where the paths 
meet, she standeth; 
Beside the gates, at the entry of the city, At the coming 
in of the doors, she erieth aloud: 
Receive instruction, and not silver; And knowledge rather 
than choice gold. 
For wisdom is better than rubies; And all things that 
may be desired are not to be compared unto her. 
Behold the fear of the Lord, that is wisdom, And to de- 
part from evil is understanding.

Pe ING CODE FOR THE HONOR 



































PEEPLES 






















TRADITION AT THE OREGON NORMAL 


1. The Honor Tradition is distinctly 
of, by and for the students of the Oregon Normal 
School; a student movement, not a faculty function. 


It is for the purpose of engendering and perpetuating 
an attitude of honor among all students of this school in regard to all things, 
but specifically in examinations. It assumes that cheating or helping to 
cheat in any form is essentially a dishonorable thing and an anomaly in a 
student, in a school the character of the Oregon Normal School. 


. It is not an honor system but a much-needed tradition 


in the making. Itis a movement, not a law; a spirit —an attitude —not a 


form or system. 


_ It is not a theory, but a working plan for accomplishing 


the purpose stated above, and any helpful suggestions or modifications of its 


plan are desired and welcomed. 


5. Its success depends upon its being a student-body 
movement, backed, not by organization merely, but by individuals. If a few, 
not big enough to follow it, continue to cheat, it is still a success if it changes or 
strengthens the attitude and practice of the rest toward all forms of dishonesty 


in examination. 


ee - 
3 | eae 
1. Abstaining from all forms of cheating in classrooms, during ex- ey 


aminations or at any other time. 
2. Refusing to give help to another in any form in classroom or

principles

THE MULESKINNER 


He hitches up when the day is fading, 
Two mules to @ go-cart, which runs on 
two wheels 
He loads on the rations, and off he goes 
jouncing 
To the boys in the trenches; through the 
darkness he steals. 


He can handle a mule like one who's a 
master 
Of animals stubborn, ferocious, untamed. 
He can get his old cart into any old corn 
He’s just a muleskinner, for such is he | 
named. 


He can talk to his mules in a voice that’s 
ommanding ; 
st fascinating, sweet, mellow or low 
vt his word is the law; and the mules, 
understanding, 
Just throw up their ears and off they 
will go, 


Two hours or more he jogs ‘long the 
and rocks, and shrapnel 

h shell-fire and gas he’s on the job 

Ho’s up and going and looking for more. 


Once o’er a road, he can always remember 
Its bad spots, and puddies, and dangerous 
turns 
He can tell in a jiffy the course of a 
whizzer, 
For there’s no one who fas than 
muleskinner learns, 


roads are the favor 
minnies 
To alight in the hope of ge 
And one who is skitlish will 
jimmies 
From the bursting of 3 
deafen noise 


Then in the blackness he halls at the 
entrance 
Of camouflaged dugout, 
ground 
They in the holo know not of his presence, 


For even the mules let out not a sound 


ero he {a for a carrying partly 


To come from their shelters ani take 
off the load 

Hero he greets them with words kind 
hearty ; 

Onco again ho starts over tho road, 


Thera ho sits. cool and collected 
Directing hia path With & touch of w rein; 
Back to his station his mules are directed 
And in the corral he re de them with 
grain, 


Then, «of himself—for Nature compels it— 
He thinks, for the first time in all these 
houra. 
Sleepy and tired, ho crawla in hia blanket 
ra shelter, protected from shower 
Heigh, ho! Amothor night passing! 
Somehow to him they eeom the 
Daylight comes creeping as ho gé 
sleeping 
Tho muleskinner fr to do it again.
